3.1. Chemical electrolyte characterization
The chemical characterization of an electrolyte plays a vital role for flow batteries. The methods in this section represent typical techniques used by synthetic and analytical chemists. They focus on the analysis of the composition of the electrolyte (solvent, supporting electrolytes, and active substances) and changes thereof, including the structure, interactions, and stability of the solvent, the redox couples, and additives. Most chemical characterization tools require operators skilled in the art, are based on stand-alone devices, often consume the sample of interest, and require thorough sample preparation. In this regard, they usually represent typical offline or ex situ characterization tools; hitherto, only spectroscopy-based methods have been occasionally applied as chemical online monitoring tools in flow batteries.

3.1.1. Nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy. 
NMR spectroscopy is an irreplaceable tool for the non-destructive and detailed investigation of chemical structures, molecular dynamics, and concentrations. Due to the complexity and size of the apparatus, its demand for energy and cooling, the general strength of the generated magnetic fields, the temperature-dependency of the measurement, and the common use of deuterated solvents for liquid NMR techniques, it is a typical offline tool. However, in situ measurements are possible using additional equipment.141–143
Its measurement principle is based on the quantization of the nuclear spin, which results in a splitting of the corresponding nuclear energy levels in an external magnetic field. As different nuclei (of the same element) vary in their local magnetic environment, the detectable resonance absorption of electromagnetic waves (radio frequencies) upon excitation of the nuclear spins to these energy levels is not equal. From this absorption spectrum, the operator can infer the chemical structure of a molecule.144 If an internal standard is used, the concentration of all nuclei in relation to the standard is quantifiable. Due to the comparably low excitation energy, the sensitivity of the technique is rather limited at ambient temperatures, leading to an unfavorable signal-to-noise-ratio. Hence, the amount of sample required for a measurement is comparably high. Although hydrogen nuclei are the most utilized reference nuclei for NMR spectroscopy, a wide range of nuclei like 13C, 31P, 15N, 17O, 19F or 51V are common.145 Several solid-state NMR techniques for batteries have been excellently reviewed.146 Liquid-based NMR techniques have only been applied to a low extend in this research field. However, the technique can provide valuable information on, e.g., electrolytes of organic-based active materials and is, thus, used for investigations of stability and structural interactions.15,142,147–152 The application of this technique for aqueous-based VRFB electrolytes has been reviewed by Choi et al.,71 whereas further examples for the use of NMR in aqueous and organic VRFB electrolytes were published elsewhere.153–157 NMR was, furthermore, used for the investigations of other metal-based RFB electrolytes as well.158


3.1.2. UV/vis spectroscopy. 
Although providing limited structural information as well, UV/vis spectroscopy is mainly used for the determination of analyte concentrations. At wavelengths ranging from approximately 200 to 1000 nm, the photon energy is sufficient to excite a valence electron from an occupied molecular orbital to an unoccupied one. After a linear calibration of the device using a set of solutions of known concentrations, this absorption process can be utilized to determine the concentration of a (optically thin) solution by the Beer–Lambert law. The simplicity of the method and its non-destructive nature enable an easy implementation as an online tool (see Fig. 4). It is widely applied for concentration and SOC measurements in vanadium-based159–195 and organic-based RFB electrolytes.12,196 The method's sensitivity strongly depends on the extinction coefficient. Many electrolyte compositions show strong absorbances for the wavelength of interest and, consequently, require adequate dilution or the employment of extremely thin cuvettes.159,163,168,172–175,177 Another problem may be the interaction of solution species by, e.g., complexation, which can cause a strongly nonlinear dependence.161,165
 Fig. 4  (a) Schematic representation of a typical UV/vis spectroscopy setup. (b) Typical UV/vis spectra for an organic electrolyte based on 9,10-anthraquinone-2,7-disulfonic acid (AQDS). Reproduced from Tong et al.12 with permission from The Royal Society of Chemistry. 


3.1.3. Infrared (IR) and Raman spectroscopy. 
IR spectroscopy can be equally suited for the determination of concentrations in RFB electrolytes. Furthermore, with wavelengths ranging from 750 nm to about one millimeter, IR radiation is capable of exciting rotational and vibrational modes of a molecular dipole. Due to these modes, IR spectra provide significant structural information suitable for the investigation of the composition and the stability of the electrolyte system as well as interactions within it, as exemplified by Herr et al.176 The method is used for concentration-dependent measurements, such as SOC monitoring, as well (Section 4.1.4).197,198
Although having a different physical background, Raman spectroscopy enables similar structural investigations which are often complementary to those obtained by IR spectroscopy. The method relies on the measurement of energy differences between scattered light and the incident beam of a monochromatic source (typically a laser). It is frequently employed for the investigation of solid surfaces (like electrodes or membranes), but has also been applied for the SOC determination199 as well as to gain information about the molecular structure of VRFB electrolytes.140,200,201


3.1.4. Mass spectrometry (MS). 
Mass spectrometry is based on the measurement of the ratio of mass and ionic charge of a molecular or atomic particle. For the measurement, the sample is first ionized. Subsequently, the generated ions are accelerated using an electrical field and are separated in a magnetic field. A detector then quantifies the portion of a certain mass increment. In the process of ionization, the molecular bonds tend to break (fragmentation), generating further information about the molecular structure of the analyte. However, for substance mixtures, the fragmentation process adds complexity to the data, making it difficult to assign a fragment to a specific substance. Therefore, the value of the method can be drastically increased by the addition of a substance separation step, commonly realized by installing a chromatography column (gas chromatography (GC) or liquid chromatography (LC)) prior to the ionizer. Column and carrier medium have to be suitable for the substance of interest.
Combinations of electrochemical cells and mass spectrometric analyzers are commonly used in analytical chemistry, which may be evidenced by the numerous reviews and book chapters about historical backgrounds,202 general aspects,203 ionization techniques,204 and cell designs.205 The available methods include direct couplings of electrochemical cells with MS devices as well as couplings with chromatography- and membrane-based separators prior to the MS device. This enables the detection of short-lived intermediates, by-products, or adducts formed during the redox reactions at the electrodes. Typically, measurements are performed in customized cells developed for this purpose, which allow for an easy and fast sampling;206–208 however, a manual transfer with syringes is used as well.209 Drawbacks of these MS-methods are high equipment costs, limitations to electrolytes suitable for the selected ionization technique, background noise due to the supporting electrolyte, and unwanted deposits in the MS device. Nevertheless, there have been applications of these techniques to electrolytes promising for flow batteries.206,210 Another MS-based technique of interest for RFB electrolyte investigations may be the inductively coupled plasma (ICP)-MS setup. For this technique, the electrolyte is evaporated in a high-temperature plasma, ionizing the sample completely. This technique is especially interesting for metal-based active materials and enables the quantitation of the active material and impurities. It was recently used for the trace metal analysis of a VRFB electrolyte211 and the determination of cerium in a Zn/Ce-RFB electrolyte.212 However, it is rarely used and competes with methods producing similar results such as Atomic Absorption Spectroscopy (AAS) and Atomic Emission Spectroscopy (AES).


3.1.5. Atomic absorption spectroscopy (AAS) and atomic emission spectroscopy (AES). 
Atomic absorption spectroscopy (AAS) and atomic emission spectroscopy (AES) (also called optical emission spectroscopy, OES) represent methods that can produce quantitative results about the elemental composition of a sample. They are in particular valuable for samples containing metal ions or metalloids and are frequently used for trace metal analyses.213 The methods are based on the atomization of the sample using high temperatures, generated by various means. The excited elements then produce a characteristic spectrum of specific wavelengths. In AAS, light is absorbed by the generated atoms, while AES systems use the light emitted by the atoms and ions excited at the very high temperatures. These temperatures are created by electrical discharges or an ICP. Using these techniques, only the total amount of an element in a sample is detectable, regardless of its redox state in the electrolyte used. ICP-AES was used for investigations of VRFB electrolytes.214–216


3.1.6. Electron Spin Resonance (ESR) spectroscopy. 
ESR (or electron paramagnetic resonance, EPR) spectroscopy is comparable to the NMR technique, but relies on the spin of unpaired electrons in a sample. It is, therefore, sensitive to organic radicals, such as aminoxyl radicals, as well as many d-group metals in various oxidation states. The method is frequently applied for the characterization of solid materials (e.g., in batteries having solid active materials217) but is applicable to liquid samples as well. EPR measurements of liquid electrolytes have been reported in metal-based electrolytes, preferably for membrane permeability tests in VRFBs,218–220 but also for structure and stability investigations.221,222 Similarly, the stability of organic-based active materials in various electrolyte systems were examined.223–226 It was utilized for SOC measurement as well (Section 4.1.4).197,227,228 Due to the low signal-to-noise ratio, the measurement error can be comparably large. Furthermore, interpretations of ESR spectra can be difficult for substances with several unpaired electrons (e.g., high-spin metal complexes) or for interactions with nuclear spins.


3.1.7. Redox titrations. 
Titrations are typical offline tools to determine concentrations of a redox-active species in solution and are, consequently, suited for FB electrolyte characterizations. A titrant solution of known concentration, which contains molecules undergoing a defined reaction with the analyte, is added to an analyte solution. The concentration of the analyte can be derived from the exact titrant volume added until the equilibrium point of the reaction is reached. Commonly applied titration techniques include cerimetry, iodometry, and permanganometry. The equilibrium point is either indicated by a color change of the solution or a potential change measured versus a reference electrode (potentiometry). Such titrations are performed for inorganic active materials as well as their organic counterparts.150,168,169,184,229–233 Furthermore, other titration techniques such as acid–base titrations are used for basic electrolyte characterizations.234–236 Titrations consume the analyte and, thus, represent typical ex situ tools. The detection error varies strongly and depends on the titration technique, the concentration of the titrant, the operator (when the equivalence point is detected by eye only), and the utilized equipment. Despite that, it is a reliable tool applied, e.g., for the ex situ SOC and SOH analysis of FB electrolytes (see Sections 4.1.2 and 4.2).


3.1.8. Elemental analysis (EA). 
EA represents a classical characterization tool for the composition analysis of organic compounds. The conventional EA starts with the chemical combustion of the sample material using pure oxygen and is followed by the separation of the generated volatile oxides using a gas chromatograph. The sample is, thus, completely consumed. EA is strongly limited to the analysis of organic materials. Some inorganic ions may indeed interfere with the measurement, which makes additives and catalysts mandatory for the measurement of FB electrolytes containing ions that are commonly used for supporting electrolytes (e.g., sodium).237 Furthermore, the substance mass has to be precisely known and, therefore, samples are generally provided as dry solids. Consequently, any FB electrolyte sample needs to be solvent-free prior to analysis. Nevertheless, especially for electrolytes based on organic active materials or supporting electrolytes, the method may provide valuable information. In this context, for example, Heo et al. used EA to determine the residual organic content in a VRFB electrolyte.211

3.2. Electrochemical methods
In contrast to the purely chemical characterization techniques, electrochemical methods rely on the measurement of voltages and currents to characterize a FB electrolyte. They require electrodes to be in direct contact with the electrolyte solution – either in situ in flow batteries or ex situ in specialized electrochemical cells. An electrochemical cell usually consists of a vessel (in some cases divided in sections connected by selective second order conductors) and working (WE), reference (RE), and counter electrodes (CE). The electric potential of the WE is usually controlled with reference to the RE. At the WE, the electrochemical reaction of interest is taking place and, thus, the WE has to be inert with respect to any electrolyte component within the applied potential range. The processes at the CE are counter-balancing the number of electrons transferred during the reaction at the WE. These processes can involve double layer charging, active substance reduction/oxidation, or parasitic reactions such as solvent electrolysis. Any included RE should have a stable potential in the utilized solution in order to enable defined control of the WE's potential. For the investigation of FBs, REs are utilized to assess individual half-cell and electrolyte parameters.238–240 Examples for standard REs include Ag/AgCl and Hg/HgCl2 electrodes for aqueous systems and Ag/AgNO3 electrodes for non-aqueous systems. Regardless of the cell setup, not only the electrolyte itself, but also the cell geometry and the electrode–electrolyte interface have a significant impact on the system's response to an electrical perturbation via overvoltage contribution to the voltage response of a cell (see Section 2.4). The current signal can be generally divided into a faradaic component, which involves the electron transfer processes between electrode and electrolyte, and a non-faradaic component, which stems from capacitive processes in the electrochemical double layer.129 With regard to this, flow batteries are two-electrode electrochemical cells in which most of the current is carried by faradaic processes, while comparably low non-faradaic currents occur. Different electrochemical techniques can be employed to obtain technical and physicochemical parameters of, e.g., the battery as a whole, the electrode–electrolyte interface, the membranes, and the electrolyte. An overview of important electrochemical characterization techniques is provided in the following subsections.

3.2.1. Cell cycling. 
The simplest electrochemical in situ characterization method of a FB is represented by cell cycling. It is usually performed in two modes: galvanostatic (constant current) or potentiostatic (constant potential) cycling. Although constant current/voltage profiles prevail in FB research, any current or voltage signal can be applied (e.g., constant power modes). In both cycling modes, the total battery capacity, QB, or the energy capacity, WB, can be obtained (see Table 1). Using the capacities of successive charge and discharge half-cycles, the coulombic efficiency, CE, the voltage efficiency, VE, and the energy efficiency, EE, for each cycle can be calculated. Additionally, using the coulomb-counting method, SOC and SOH values can be estimated (Sections 4.1 and 4.2). Specifically designed battery cycling experiments enable the assessment of, e.g., utilization factors, cycle as well as calendar lifetimes, charge/discharge times, capacity fade rates, and response times. In particular for long-term stability investigations in RFBs, cell cycling represents an irreplaceable tool (Sections 3.2 and 4.3). Despite that, full cell cycling experiments are currently increasingly criticized because of several intrinsic downsides.15,23,43 The most striking disadvantage is the lack of sufficient information on half-cell or electrolyte stability, which makes it highly difficult to unravel specific causes for capacity fade rates. In order to obtain satisfactory half-cell information in RFBs, Darling and Perry were the first to apply a symmetrical cell setup utilizing only the electrolyte of interest in the both half-cells (Fig. 5),241 which was subsequently advanced and successfully applied for other electrolytes.15,108,242–245 Symmetric cell setups decrease the impact of crossover in the FB, while still exposing the active material to cycling conditions.43 A comprehensive overview of setups for cell cycling was given by Nölle et al., recently.246
 Fig. 5  Schematic representation of an unbalanced compositionally symmetric cell setup. Both half-cells comprise the same electrolyte, initially charged to 50% SOC, with different capacities, as defined by their electrolyte volumes. 
Despite its advantages, cell cycling always records all processes leading to a current or voltage response, regardless of their origin. Other techniques might be applied, in order to obtain a deeper insight into the specific electrochemical processes involved.


3.2.2. Direct current (DC) techniques for overvoltage analyses. 
Several methods have been developed that deal with the investigation of the overvoltages (Section 2.4.2) within a flow cell and the quantification of its components247,248 as well as the additional complications of analysis by non-faradaic currents.249 Among the methods which enable the assessment of overvoltages are DC techniques (e.g., polarization curves or interruptive measurements) as well as AC-based techniques (e.g., impedance spectroscopy). Simple polarization curve experiments rely on the measurement of voltage–current curves.37,247,250 As mentioned earlier (Section 2.4.2), all the contributions to the overall overvoltage in the cell occur to different extents and prevail at specific zones of the polarization curve (Fig. 6). Linear parts in the current–voltage dependency indicate ohmic losses, while non-linear behaviors in the high current regions can be attributed mostly to mass-transport limitations and a non-linear voltage–current development in the low current region is commonly assigned to the activation resistance of the redox reactions.37 The recorded data are generally plotted as voltage over current density or as the product of both (power density) over the normalized current. Typically, polarization measurements are used for the basic assessment and estimation of limiting overpotential contributions.37,247 Furthermore, they are used to measure the maximum output power at a given SOC and design of the cell. Closely related to polarization curve measurements are interruptive techniques, such as current interruption (CI) and the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). During cycling of a cell, the applied current is switched off, resulting in a time-dependent voltage drop. The main part of this voltage drop is caused by ohmic and activation resistances. They are responsible for the almost immediate overvoltage drop after the current interruption, while the rest of the overvoltage continuously decays over time and is caused in large part by the concentration overpotentials at the electrodes.251 For GITT, this process is periodically repeated within a charging/discharging segment of a battery cycling experiment. It is commonly used in conventional batteries for the measurement of diffusion coefficients or the determination of the equilibrium potentials at different SOCs. However, it may only provide limited information for flow batteries due to their increased mass transport to the electrode during operation.251 Although an overall estimation of the overvoltage may be possible, a general quantification of the individual contributions cannot be attained easily by CI techniques.37 More sophisticated methods like the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) are required for that.
 Fig. 6  Schematic representation of a polarization curve with the contribution of different overvoltages in a flow cell.5 


3.2.3. Electrical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). 
With the EIS technique, one can differentiate between the different time-dependent components contributing to the overall cell resistance. It is commonly applied in battery research in general252 and FB electrolyte parameters may similarly be obtained in separate measurement cells. There are two measurement modes: while in PEIS (potentiostatic EIS) an AC voltage signal for system perturbation is applied and the current response is observed, the GEIS (galvanostatic EIS) technique works vice versa. During the measurement, the frequency of the AC signal is varied. Both modes usually generate equal results if the electrochemical properties of the system do not change over the measurement time. Since the causes for the different overvoltage contributions evolve on different time scales, the measured response to the AC perturbation signal is frequency dependent. Generally, for systems that evolve slowly in time (e.g., batteries), the frequency of the AC perturbation is varied one by one. However, if systems do not show large faradaic currents, long EIS measurements can become inaccurate.253 For these systems, techniques comprising a mathematical transformation have been developed which rely on an excitation signal of superimposed frequencies. A Fourier transformation of the response signal then provides the desired frequency domain. This method exhibits measurement times as low as several milliseconds.249,254 For the analysis, the battery is usually approximated by an equivalent circuit model composed of resistors, capacitors, inductors, and specific electrochemical elements. These elements represent the physicochemical processes in the battery and their values are obtained by fitting the mathematical equation behind the circuit model to the experimental impedance spectra.129 In particular, impedance values for the activation and ohmic resistances as well as the double-layer capacitances can be directly obtained.255 For visualization, the impedance values are either displayed as a function of frequency (Bode plot) or using the real and imaginary part of the impedance (Nyquist plot).


3.2.4. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and rotating disc electrode (RDE). 
Potential sweep and step methods represent some of the fundamental techniques in electrochemistry and are, thus, highly suitable for the assessment of FB electrolyte parameters. Two of the most frequently applied methods in FB research are the CV and RDE measurements. However, both were reviewed as diagnostic tools before and, thus, we only provide basic information in this section and refer to the respective literature for deeper insights.37,129,256–258
Cyclic voltammetry is a wide-spread, easily implementable, and fast method, which is frequently applied to characterize electrolytes.259 In the commonly utilized three-electrode-setup, the potential of the WE (versus the RE) is linearly swept back and forth in a defined potential range at various and usually constant (potential) scan rates. The resulting current response from the sample electrolyte is composed of non-faradaic/capacitive and faradaic components. The non-faradaic currents are usually compensated during data analysis by subtraction of an initially recorded scan in the electrolyte prior to adding the redox-active substance (“blank”). The faradaic processes, on the other hand, cause the typical, peak-shaped signal in the voltammogram: in equilibrium, the ion concentrations throughout the solution are virtually constant. At sufficiently high potentials, however, the redox couple reacts at the electrode's surface causing a deviation from the equilibrium concentration distribution. Diffusive mass transport of unreacted ions to the surface is, subsequently, driven by the increasing concentration gradient between electrode surface and bulk electrolyte. At the peak of the curve, this mass transport becomes the rate-determining step of the reaction. A further increase of the potential, consequently, has no effect on the current response of the system. Instead, the current monotonically decreases due to the continuous expansion of the diffusion layer. Upon reversal of the scan direction, the same processes take place for the other redox-species resulting in a second peak.259 The resulting voltammograms enable the evaluation of the electrochemical reversibility of the reaction: for an ideally reversible reaction, for example, the ratio of cathodic and anodic peak currents equals one, while the peaks show a separation of 57/ze mV. Furthermore, the peak currents are directly proportional to the square root of the scan rate (see Randles–Sevcik equation260,261) and the recorded peak potentials do not vary significantly with the scan rate. Irreversible or quasi-reversible redox reactions are indicated by specific deviations from these ideal values. However, even for virtually reversible reactions the experimentally observed peak separation may be higher than 57/ze mV if non-ideal experimental conditions are applied. For example, both the measured peak current ratio and the peak separation may contain errors due to the non-faradaic currents and the so-called IR drop.262 If careful experimentation is performed, however, the reaction rate constant of the electrochemical reaction (Nicholson method), the diffusion coefficient of the redox species (Randles–Sevcik/Cottrell equation), the number of electrons transferred in the reaction (ze), and the concentration can be estimated.129,259
Another commonly applied technique to obtain fundamental parameters of the redox couple is the rotating disc electrode voltammetry. The WE in this method is configured as a rotating disk-shaped electrode (see Fig. 7a). With the rotation of the WE at a defined, constant rate, a laminar convection of the electrolyte to the electrode surface is induced, which causes a steady concentration profile of active species at the electrode surface. At sufficiently high voltages, where mass-transfer limiting conditions prevail at the electrode surface, limiting currents are measured (Fig. 7b). The technique is frequently employed for the measurement of rate constants and diffusion coefficients of the redox couples by using the Levich and Koutecky–Levich analysis (Fig. 7c).129,229,263
 Fig. 7  (a) Schematic representation of an RDE setup. Shown is the typical three-electrode cell with the working electrode implemented as a rotating disc electrode. (b) RDE voltammograms of the organic N,N,N-2,2,6,6-heptamethylpiperidinyl oxy-4-ammonium chloride (TEMPTMA) active material. Note that the voltammogram at 0 rpm resembles the normal cyclic voltammogram of the compound.1 (Copyright©2016, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA.) (c) Levich plot of the obtained RDE data. The linear slope contains the diffusion coefficient, as determinable by the Levich-equation.1 (Copyright©2016, Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA.) 
Other commonly encountered methods include staircase and sinusoidal voltammetry as well as pulsed techniques such as square-wave voltammetry. They usually allow a deeper insight into the electrochemical processes occurring at the electrode, e.g., the discrimination of capacitive and faradaic contributions in a voltammogram.249,264


3.2.5. Spectroelectrochemical methods. 
Apart from the presented chemical methods and electrochemical diagnostic tools, combinations of both are frequently employed as well. Combinations of chemical and electrochemical tools include couplings of electrochemical measurements with spectroscopic techniques (spectroelectrochemistry), such as UV/vis, IR, Raman or NMR as well as MS couplings.265 The most prominent example is the commonly applied combination of voltammetric techniques and UV/vis spectroscopy. Such measurements are usually used for the investigation of the redox-active materials in batteries and are especially valuable for the instantaneous detection and characterization of short-lived intermediates.266,267 In FBs, spectroelectrochemical measurements have been exploited, e.g., for the characterization of organic substances,268–270 metal-based271–274 as well as other electrolytes.275 However, again we would like to refer to the respective literature for further insight into the utilization of these techniques.265,276,277

3.3. pH value
In both inorganic and organic aqueous flow batteries, the pH value of the electrolyte and its variation during (dis-)charging can fundamentally influence the reaction of the redox couple. The pH value is defined as the negative logarithm of the hydrogen ion activity (see eqn (2.34)) and, in general, it's value is temperature-dependent and can vary strongly during flow battery charge and discharge processes. The pH value is only defined for water-based electrolytes and, as a consequence, only has an impact on aqueous FBs. However, a large part of FB research and commercialized systems employ water as an electrolyte base due to its high electrolyte conductivity and low cost. In the current FB literature, a pH range from −0.24 to 14 is encountered in various electrolyte compositions. Due to the high hydrogen and hydroxide ion mobilities, pH alterations can cause a variation of the electrolyte conductivity; due to this, batteries with extreme pH values often exhibit higher conductivities. In addition to that, the pH can affect the stability of the redox-active substances,95,245,278–280 their solubility,147,281,282 the general stability of the electrolyte,283,284 the redox potential of the redox-active species,285–289 the electrolyte corrosivity, and the environmental safety.290–292 In terms of the technical parameters, the pH value influences the VE, EE, Qn, Un, Wn, Lcal, and Lcyc. Therefore, its measurement is essential for an optimal and stable battery operation. The number of approaches to measure a solution's pH is large. We will, therefore, cover only the most common methods here.

3.3.1. Harned cell. 
This is the standard method suggested by the IUPAC. The measurement is performed in a so-called Harned cell using the following electrode configuration:293Pt|H2|buffer, solution of interest, Cl−|AgCl|Ag.While the method is a standard procedure for pH measurement, it is uncomfortable to use. In particular, the hydrogen flow can contaminate the analyte during the investigation.


3.3.2. Glass electrode. 
The glass electrode (Fig. 8a) relies on the measurement of junction potential differences across a glass membrane, which selectively interacts with the protons in the analyte solution. The setup uses the following configuration:2,129Ag|AgCl|Cl−‖solution of interest‖Cl−|AgCl|Ag,The glass membrane is placed between the two reference electrodes and is the only source of potential change in the system. Due to hydration of a thin layer of the silicate glass at the interface to the analyte solution, the voltage differences measured in different analyte solutions mainly originates from the varying proton activities in the analyte solutions. However, the absolute voltage value also depends on the specific setup and, consequently, a calibration in standard solutions is always required. Furthermore, several minutes are required to stabilize the voltage signal, the glass bulb is rather fragile, and drifts in the Ag/AgCl potential necessitate frequent recalibrations. Despite that, glass electrodes are comparably cheap, measurements are comfortable to perform, and they exhibit a high precision in a large pH range from 0 to 12. Beyond this range, special glass compositions should be applied in order to keep proton selectivity high.
 Fig. 8  (a) Glass electrode pH sensor. (b) SiO2-based ISFET transistor-based pH sensor.2 (Copyright©2016 Elsevier Ltd.) 


3.3.3. Transistor-based sensors. 
In the transistor-based methods (Fig. 8b) an ion-selective layer (e.g., SiO2, Si3N4Ox, Al2O3 and Ta2O5)2,294 is coated on the gate contact of a conventional metal oxide semiconductor field effect transistor (MOSFET), which is in direct contact with the analyte solution. Interaction of this layer with the protons in the solution causes an additional junction potential at the gate. This adds up to a bias voltage, which is applied between a RE in the solution and the source contact of the transistor. In response to this, the measured current flow between source and drain changes. The change of the gate voltage is, thus, directly related to the differences of pH values in different solutions. As a consequence, the current flow through the transistor can be utilized to assess the pH value of the solution after a suitable calibration. Various configurations can be used: ion-sensitive field effect transistor (ISFET), where the semiconductor between source and drain interfaces the sample solution through an ion-selective layer; extended gate field effect transistor (EGFET), where the gate is connected to an electrode coated with the ion-selective layer and immersed in the solution; interdigitated extended gate field effect transistor (IEGFET) that operates on the same principle as the EGFET, though the surface of the sensing electrode is significantly larger.2,294,295 Such sensors are mechanically robust, possess faster response times compared to glass electrodes, and have significantly smaller sizes. On the other hand, the stability of the ion-selective layer is limited, and they come with high costs.


3.3.4. Metal–metal oxide sensors. 
For these sensors, a metal oxide layer is coated on the surface of a metal electrode as an ion-sensitive layer. Among the well-studied oxides are TiO2, RuO2, RhO2, Ta2O5, IrO2, PtO2, and ZrO2.296,297 The pH value is determined by the measurement of the equilibrium potential of the metal oxide-coated electrode versus a reference electrode with a stable potential. Such sensors are mechanically robust, can be easily miniaturized, and can provide proton sensitivity superior to glass electrodes. However, they usually struggle with a lower range of stability, which is determined by the metal oxide, and their potential drift necessitates frequent recalibration.


3.3.5. Resistance measurement. 
Two electrical contacts are connected with a material, which exchanges protons with the solution of interest and, subsequently, changes its conductivity (chemiresistor).295 Such systems do not require a RE and, as a consequence, potential drift is avoided. In addition to that, their size can be even smaller than transistor-based and metal–metal oxide sensors. Gou et al. demonstrated a variant that covered a pH range from 2 to 12 and was stable for at least 120 days.298 Despite its interesting and promising approach, this sensor type represents an emerging technology and, hence, the attainable pH range, the sensitivity, and the selectivity require further evaluation.


3.3.6. Dyes and optical analysis. 
The method is based on the selective interaction of protons with various available dyes, which cause a detectable color change in the electrolyte. If combined with an optical sensor, the pH value can be readily evaluated. However, for each of the dyes, the operable pH range is restricted due to their limited sensitivity and stability at the different acidities. Additionally, the respective dyes have to be added to the sample electrolyte; hence, this method is mainly suited for ex situ characterization.297


3.3.7. Applications in FB literature. 
In FB literature, the electrolyte pH value is often estimated by means of a glass electrode150,245,279,284,286,287,292,299–306 that sometimes can be combined with pH metric paper, i.e., organic dyes.147 The electrolyte pH monitoring inside a FB is usually performed via placing probes in the electrolyte tanks and performing either continuous measurement during the complete cycling period, at discrete SOC values, or in between two cycles.95,283,285
Unfortunately, in most publications the information on the pH measurement methodology is either not stated at all95,268,278,280–283,285,288,290,291,307–320 or the sensor type is not specified.110,147,321
Based on the available information, the most appropriate approaches for pH characterization in FBs seem to be glass electrodes and transistor-based methods. Both are readily available and represent commercialized technologies, which can provide precise results in a broad pH range, are chemically stable, and highly sensitive. Compared to glass electrodes, transistor-based sensors are smaller, have faster response times, and are mechanically more robust. Consequently, they are easier to use for monitoring in a laboratory FB. With regard to industrial application, such sensors can be placed in the pipeline before and/or after the flow cell and provide broader information on the electrolyte condition compared to sensors placed in the tanks (where huge pH value gradients might prevail due to insufficient mixing). However, the potential drift of both sensor types requires frequent recalibration, which is a significant issue for online monitoring. Such drawbacks might be overcome by resistance-based sensors in the future.

3.4. Conductivity
The electrical conductivity of an electrolyte, κ, originates from the movement of all ions in it (see eqn (2.36)). With regard to this, for example, the system temperature, the type of solvent, the amount as well as type of supporting electrolytes, the concentration and number of ionic charges of the redox species in all its redox states, the corresponding diffusivities of the ions, and the viscosity of the solution have a significant impact on the electrolyte conductivity. Typical values range from 1 to 39 mS cm−1 in organic solvents (25 °C),242,322–326 from 50 to 560 mS cm−1 (25 °C) in aqueous solvents,105,140,159,194,327–348 and from 0.7 to 25 mS cm−1 (80 °C) in deep eutectic electrolytes.349
A high electrolyte conductivity is desired to lower the ohmic overvoltages within the battery. As a consequence, the conductivity has a crucial effect on the overall battery u, VE, and, subsequently, an impact on EE and EERT. Furthermore, since the ohmic resistance causes heat dissipation during operation, a high conductivity also decreases the effect of the flow cell's self-heating. The electrolyte conductivity also varies with the electrolyte's SOC, due to the oxidation/reduction of redox species and the mass transfer of the supporting electrolyte ions through the membrane. In cases where the conductivity-SOC dependency is known (and calibrated), electrolyte conductivity measurements enable electrolyte SOC determination (see Section 4.1.5).
There is a multitude of setups and methods for conductivity measurements.350 Among the widely used devices which do not alter the electrolyte composition, there are the so-called contact and inductive (contactless) sensors.351 A common feature of both approaches is that every conductance measurement cell has its own cell constant, Kcell. This device-specific value is a proportionality factor which includes influences of the geometric dimensions and arrangement of the electrodes used for the conductivity measurement. In order to obtain the cell constant, every conductometer needs to be calibrated in reference solutions with known specific conductivities or with respect to other conductometers with a known cell constant.352,353 Various calibration techniques can be employed; in general, each of them has its precision limits. These techniques were recently compared in detail by, e.g., Seitz et al.352 Contact sensors are using a two- or four-electrode (two- or four-pole) setup that is immersed in the analyte solution (Fig. 9a and b).351 AC signals are often applied in order to avoid solution electrolysis. In dilute solutions, i.e., in systems with low conductivity, where electrode polarization effects can be neglected, two-electrode contact sensors can be applied. The circuit measures both the total current, I, and the voltage drop, U, between the electrodes. This kind of sensors can be employed in the range of specific conductivities from 10−5 to 102 mS cm−1.351 In order to keep an acceptable precision at higher conductivities, the current applied between the electrodes should be increased. However, this causes additional electrode polarization that can significantly disturb the results of a measurement. In particular for flow battery electrolytes, this interference mainly originates from redox reactions and other electrochemical processes. In such cases four-electrode cells should be used. Here, the current is applied between one pair of electrodes and the voltage is measured between a second pair. This approach mitigates polarization effects and enables the measurement of conductivities higher than 102 mS cm−1 with an acceptable precision.
 Fig. 9  (a) Two electrode (pole) contact conductivity sensor. (b) Four electrode (pole) contact conductivity sensor. (c) Inductive (toroidal) non-contact conductivity sensor. 
One should keep in mind that the measurement remains reliable as long as the positions of the electrodes in the cell, their surface, shape, and composition does not change (i.e., the cell constant is a fixed value). Hence corrosive media, electrode fouling, and high flow rates should be avoided in order to not damage the sensor chemically or mechanically. Otherwise, frequent recalibration is required to obtain reliable values.
Inductive sensors (or “toroidal” or “electrodeless”), are usually not in direct contact with the measured solution (Fig. 9c).351,354 The sensor consists of two parallel and closely aligned inductive coils. Wrapped up in a dielectric material that prevents corrosion (e.g., a polymer), the setup is immersed into the solution. On application of an alternating current at the first coil, a magnetic field is generated, which induces a current in the electrolyte. In turn, this current induces another magnetic field that penetrates the second coil and induces a current there, which is measured and used to calculate the electrolyte conductivity.
It should be noted that the sensor's signal can be strongly affected by walls (e.g., the pipeline) and, thus, the sensor should always be positioned at least 30 mm away from solid boundaries.354,355 However, in cases where the position of the sensor remains absolutely constant, a preliminary calibration usually suffices to eliminate such disturbances. As for contact cells, a calibration procedure is always required. Furthermore, the magnetic field resulting from the current flow in the electrolyte solution needs to exceed the sensitivity limits of the receiver sensor. Therefore, inductive conductometers can only be used for the measurement in electrolytes with specific conductivities higher than 0.015 mS cm−1.351,355 Despite that, the absence of a direct contact between the sensor and the electrolyte represents a significant advantage in comparison to two-pole contact conductometers, where even small corrosion or fouling artefacts significantly disturb the results. With regard to that, however, four-pole contact cells are more reliable due to the low currents measured between the voltage-measuring electrodes. Toroidal sensors are resistant to corrosion and any kind of fouling will not significantly disturb the conductivity measurement as long as its inner diameter and inductance is not varying strongly.354
In general, conductivity sensors comprise a probe and a signal analyzing device. Unfortunately, in most cases only the analyzing device is stated without proper information on the utilized probe in the FB literature.194,327,329–331,333–337,339,340,342,343,347–349 Due to this, it can be quite problematic to compare conductivity values from different studies.159,328,348 If one, for example, compares conductivities of compositionally identical VRFB electrolytes, deviations of up to 53% can be found for the reported specific conductivities (see Table 2). In two of the three resources, the kind of probe is not stated and, hence, it is impossible to judge what the cause of the different reported values is and which one is more reliable.
Of course, the required precision of a measurement depends on the specific research task. In some studies, EIS inside a flow cell was employed for conductivity measurements (see Section 3.2.3).332,356 However, a flow cell is prone to changes in the surface characteristics of the carbon felt, membrane fouling, and polarization effects during cycling and, thus, it can be difficult to obtain reliable in situ electrolyte conductivity values without disturbance from other components.
In flow battery research, contact cells for electrolyte conductivity measurements currently prevail.105,194,242,322,324,326–331,333–340,342–346,349 Two-electrode sensors are often applied to investigate electrolytes with conductivities below 102 mS cm−1.242,322,324,326 However, some studies applied such sensors in electrolytes with significantly higher conductivites,338 although four-electrode sensors deliver significantly higher precisions at high conductivities and should be used in such electrolytes. To the best of our knowledge, there are no cases reported when inductive sensors were employed for laboratory applications in FB literature. This might be due to their rather large size (e.g., the Mettler Toledo inductive sensor InPro® 7250 Series has dimensions of 12 cm in length, 4.8 cm in width, and 2.9 cm in height). Furthermore, the device itself is more expensive and readings can strongly depend on the environment of the sensor position. In contrast to other conductometers, which can be calibrated ex situ, inductive conductometers, therefore, require a more complicated in situ calibration. As a consequence, contact sensors are much more comfortable for laboratory applications. In most of the reports, the investigations are performed on small time scales and the sensors can be recalibrated between the readings, even if the surface is fouled or damaged chemically. However, in industrial applications and for continuous in situ monitoring, the stability of an inductive sensor might be more viable, while its large size does not seem critical when industrial tanks and pipelines are considered.
3.5. Viscosity
The simplest definition of the dynamic or absolute viscosity, μ, relies on the intramolecular, frictional forces that counteract the flow of a fluid (see eqn (2.37)). While μ is a scalar constant for isotropic Newtonian fluids, it can also represent a velocity-dependent tensor in more complex cases (e.g., non-Newtonian fluids). In hydrodynamic calculations, it is common to use the kinematic viscosity, υ ≡ μρ−1, where ρ is the density of the fluid. The viscosity changes during FB operation due to the variations in temperature and electrolyte composition (i.e., with changing SOH and SOC (see Sections 4.1 and 4.2)). The electrolyte viscosity has a significant impact on the pumping power and, hence, on VE, EE, EERT, u, and Pmax of the flow battery (see Section 2.4.2). Therefore, the investigation of the viscosity characteristics is highly important for the development of new electrolytes or electrolyte compositions as well as the design and optimization of flow cells for specific electrolytes. The latter is particularly important with respect to non-Newtonian electrolytes, which exhibit specific responses to the different shear stresses within a flow battery system.
The various FB electrolytes reported so far cover a wide range of different viscosity values: aqueous electrolytes range from 1.0 mPa s at 25 °C to 580 mPa s at shear rate 8 s−1 at room temperature,88,90,134,151,152,174,185,189,193,194,219,232,305,314,327,330,331,334–338,348,357–369 non-aqueous electrolytes from 0.4 to 200 mPa s at 25 °C,242,322,324,370–373 aqueous suspensions from 1.0 to 27.0 mPa s at 25 °C,374,375 non-aqueous suspensions at shear rates from 7 to 100 s−1 demonstrate viscosity values from 35 to 10 mPa s or at shear rates from 10 to 100 s−1 from 4000 to 200 mPa s (temperature was not stated),376–386 liquid active substance based electrolytes have viscosities from 23 to 38 mPa s at 40 °C,387 ionic liquids around 73 mPa s at 25 °C,388 eutectics from 33.2 mPa s at 22 °C to 104 mPa s at 25 °C,324,389–391 and slightly diluted eutectics from 3.5 mPa s “at room temperature” to 130 mPa s at 25 °C.324,349,389,392 Many of the electrolytes exhibit non-Newtonian behavior and, as a consequence, it is difficult to compare the impact of these different viscosity values on the battery performance unless the flow rate and geometry are identical. A carefully chosen and thoroughly reported methodology is, therefore, mandatory. In general, the following techniques are frequently applied for viscosity measurements in solutions:

3.5.1. Capillary viscometers and rheometers. 
The most widely known types of such measurement devices are the Ostwald and Ubbelohde viscometers (Fig. 10a). A liquid is forced to pass through capillaries of a defined diameter and length. The time required for the liquid to flow through this capillary is related to the viscosity.393 The force that drives the liquid to flow through the capillary can either be solely gravitational or an externally applied pressure. This method is suitable for the viscosity measurement of Newtonian liquids due to its relatively low costs and simplicity. On the other hand, the density of the liquid of interest needs to be determined and has to stay constant within the range of pressures applied to it. Additionally, it is relatively difficult to manage the magnitude of the force that pushes a liquid through the thin capillary, which makes the method unreliable for the analysis of non-Newtonian liquids.393,394 Furthermore, the geometric characteristics of each capillary needs to be considered by calibration with a liquid of known viscosity and density393 and is essential in order to obtain reliable results. This is true, however, for all methods discussed in this section and calibration constants are usually determined based on similar approaches.
 Fig. 10  Schematic representation of a (a) capillary viscometer, a (b) rotational viscometer, and a (c) vibrating (oscillatory) viscometer.5 


3.5.2. Falling ball. 
A ball with known density and shape (mostly spherical; however, for highly viscous liquids a rod shape is used) is directed to sink vertically through the sample liquid. The time required for the ball to move over a defined distance in the liquid-filled vessel is related to the (dynamic) viscosity. The size of the ball influences the flow distribution and, hence, in order to not disturb the results and keep the movement of the ball vertical, the diameter of the ball is usually chosen to be less than a fifth of the diameter of the vessel.394,395 The equipment for the method is relatively cheap; however, a large volume of the sample liquid is needed. Furthermore, the method has a rather high measurement uncertainty and both the density of the ball and the sample have to be known accurately.393–395 The influence of the setup geometry is again handled via calibration.


3.5.3. Rolling ball. 
In contrast to the falling ball viscometer, the vessel with the sample liquid in the rolling ball viscometer is tilted and the ball rolls along the wall of the vessel. This makes the ball movement trajectory much more reproducible; however, a change in the tilt angle always necessitates a re-calibration. At the same time, the adjustment of the tilting angle enables the management of the effective shear stress and, consequently, enables the accurate analysis of non-Newtonian liquids as well.393–395


3.5.4. Rotational viscometers and rheometers. 
A rotationally symmetric body (e.g., cylinder, conus, plate) is placed inside the sample liquid or in contact with it (Fig. 10b): a precisely controlled torque is delivered to the body and its rotation speed is measured. The liquid slows down the rotation due to its viscosity and, thus, the dynamic viscosity can be calculated. This method is independent of the density of the investigated liquid, but still requires a calibration. A large range of torques and, hence, shear rates can be applied, which enables to measure even highly viscous liquids at a sufficient precision and to investigate the rheology of non-Newtonian liquids.393,394,396


3.5.5. Vibrating (oscillatory). 
A vibrational body is placed into the analyte liquid and the change of its oscillation characteristics by the liquid viscosity is analyzed (Fig. 10c). The resonance frequency, the free vibration decay, and the vibration propagation is measured.394 This approach features fast response times, applicability in online monitoring systems, and a high accuracy. On the other hand, the measurement depends on the density of the sample liquid and has a high vulnerability to changes of the oscillator mass (e.g., due to corrosion or contamination).394,395,397 The variation of the oscillation frequency and the amplitude enable the analysis of the rheological properties of non-Newtonian liquids. The advantages and limitations for investigations of non-Newtonian liquids by both vibrating and rotational techniques are comprehensively discussed, e.g., by T. Mezger.398


3.5.6. Applications in FB literature. 
All aforementioned techniques cover the whole range of viscosity values reported for FB electrolytes so far. However, the capillary and falling (or rolling) ball techniques can only measure viscosities up to 6 Pa s399 and 104 Pa s,400 respectively.
Consequently, all aforementioned methods have been applied to flow battery electrolytes. However, among the reviewed resources, suspension-based electrolytes are solely analyzed by rotational rheometers,374–386 because this method delivers a high accuracy at high viscosities. Furthermore, the presence of solid particles in a sample disturbs the results from the methods that depend on the density of a sample. With regard to this, if a non-rotational viscometer is used for the investigation of suspensions, only kinematic viscosities can be stated. Additionally, suspensions usually are non-Newtonian liquids and rotational rheometers are suited best for the investigation of such systems. There are also examples for the usage of rotational rheometers for the measurement of non-Newtonian viscosities in water-based electrolytes314,366 and in highly viscous deep eutectics324,389,390 for which the type of behavior was not specified. Capillary,90,151,152,174,189,193,194,322,327,334–338,348,365 rolling ball,88,134,359,362,363,367,401 vibrational,232,242,372,388 and falling ball368 viscometers are used for the investigation of relatively low-viscous liquids. However, exceptions exist: some aqueous vanadium electrolytes with nearly 190 mPa s and non-aqueous viologen polymer solutions with 200 mPa s were investigated by capillary viscometers.322,348 Additionally, the viscosity of an ionic liquid-based electrolyte with a value of 73 mPa s was measured by a vibrational rheometer.388 The monitoring of FB electrolytes was performed by discrete ex situ measurements between charge and discharge cycles383 or as a continuous online monitoring for SOC measurement, where the viscosity was assessed from the pressure drop over the porous electrode.88 While the authors of the latter study emphasized a limitation of viscosity measurement methods for in situ monitoring, other not FB related studies demonstrated that the appropriate choice of the viscometer type and its position in the system can provide sufficient measurement accuracies and response times for such purposes.394,396
It can be concluded that electrolytes with viscosities higher than 100 mPa s and with non-Newtonian behavior are more likely to be measured by rotational rheometers due to their precision at high viscosities and due to a higher convenience for shear stress dependent investigations. For Newtonian liquids with viscosities below 100 mPa s any of the other presented methods is suitable; however, the falling ball viscometer seems to be less preferable due to a rather high measurement uncertainty. For online viscometry, the rolling ball and vibrational viscometers represent the best options. Still, it should be kept in mind that vibrational viscometers are, in general, more vulnerable to measurement errors if they chemically interact with the flow battery electrolyte.

3.6. Density
Electrolyte density is the ratio of the sample mass to its volume. Like conductivity and viscosity, ρ is dependent on the temperature (see Section 2.4.2). The density of flow battery electrolytes lies in the range from 1.006 to 1.8 g cm−3 (20 °C) for water-based systems;14,219,232,331,337,338,340,348,357,359,362,363,365,401–403 and from 0.8 to 1.2 g cm−3 for non-aqueous systems including deep eutectic solutions.343,388,389,391,404 It plays a role in the relationship between volumetric and specific/gravimetric electrolyte capacity and, more importantly, it appears in several hydrodynamic equations. Therefore, it is an important parameter for the modelling of FB behavior.405,406 It can be stated in general, that the higher the density of an electrolyte, the higher is its inertia and the higher is the pumping power needed to drive the electrolyte circulation. Therefore, ρ has an influence on battery Pp and, consequently, on EERT. In addition to that, the density is the proportionality factor connecting kinematic and dynamic viscosity values. This is particularly relevant for viscosity measurements, as most viscometers are measuring kinematic viscosities. Due to the compositional changes in the electrolyte upon SOC variations, the density is a SOC-dependent quantity. Hence, it can be used for online SOC monitoring as mentioned for the viscosity and conductivity (see Section 4.1). The following methods for the density determination of liquids are the commonly used ones.

3.6.1. Absolute methods. 
The direct measurement of the volume as well as the mass of a sample represents the most obvious approach for density determination. However, most of the simple methods to determine the mass and the volume of solids and liquids exhibit a very limited accuracy. Nevertheless, if specific devices are applied, absolute methods can achieve the highest accuracy among the existing methods (uncertainties up to 10−5%).407 The widespread application of these accurate approaches is limited due to two reasons: firstly, the equipment is usually far too complicated for most situations and, secondly, it is only suited for solid materials, because the shape of the sample has to remain constant throughout the measurement.


3.6.2. Densimeters based on vibrating bodies. 
This method is based on the determination of the resonance frequency of a hollow cylinder (straight), wire, or tube (varied shape) contacting with a sample solution.408,409 For liquid-density measurements, an uncertainty of as low as 0.1% can be obtained by this approach. However, a previous calibration with a liquid of known density is required.


3.6.3. Buoyancy based. 
If a solid standard sample is weighted in air and, subsequently, in a liquid, where it experiences a buoyancy force counteracting gravity, the difference in the measured weight can be used to calculate its density. Different types of the buoyancy-based density measurement methods include hydrostatic weighting, pressure of flotation, magnetic suspension, and magnetic suspension combined with weighing.407,408 All the approaches are based on the same principle but operate under different pressure and temperature conditions and are suited for distinct density ranges. The uncertainty for this kind of measurements is usually around 0.1%.408


3.6.4. Applications in FB literature. 
In the FB literature, the exact experimental conditions and the applied method for density measurements are often not stated.219,324,331,338,343,391 Furthermore, only the density of solution-based electrolytes were investigated so far, most-likely due to the rather complicated density measurement in suspension-based systems. In half of the reviewed studies detailing the applied technique, an absolute method was applied,232,337,340,348,357,365,403 while for the other half vibrating-body densimeters were chosen.14,359,362,363,388,389,401,402 When comparing the densities of, e.g., similar vanadium electrolytes reported in different studies, the results are in good agreement even if different approaches were used (Table 3). Compared to the other techniques, Vibrating-body densimeters usually possess a slightly higher accuracy (as high as 0.000005 g cm−3). However, it should be noted that the sensor parts, which are brought in contact with the sample, are often made from metal in vibrating body densimeters. Since many FB electrolytes are highly corrosive and absolute methods can provide comparable accuracies (approximately 0.3% difference (Table 3) in direct comparison), absolute methods might be the better choice for this research field. At least, the material-specific limitations should be kept in mind, when choosing the method.

3.7. Temperature
One of the most important parameters for battery operation is the electrolyte temperature, because nearly all physicochemical quantities vary with it. Consequently, all electrochemical processes show strong temperature dependencies. For example, increasing the temperature usually influences the equilibrium potential of a redox couple, its mass transfer parameters, the rate constant of the redox reaction and, thus, can cause a significant reduction of the overvoltages. At the same time, however, it facilitates side reactions, species crossover, and other capacity degradation mechanisms. In turn, the electrolyte temperature itself is dependent on, e.g., the operational parameters (see Section 2.4.2). In terms of the battery technical parameters, T has a significant impact on E, W, VE, CE, EE, Pp, EERT, u, qf, Lcyc, and Lcal. At fixed operational parameters, a higher temperature increases all battery performance characteristics at the expense of a CE decrease, capacity fade facilitation, and a subsequent reduction of Lcyc and Lcal. As a consequence, a detailed knowledge of the impact of the thermal variations on all parameter levels as well as a temperature monitoring and management with help of BMS and the battery periphery is required to ensure a safe and efficient battery operation (see Section 5.2). In the investigations of FB electrolytes, an overall temperature range from −35 to 80 °C was covered so far; however, a stable battery operation is usually reported between −10 and 50 °C. Since a comprehensive review of temperature measurement techniques is provided by Childs et al.,410 we only discuss the most popular sensors in this section.411

3.7.1. Liquid in glass. 
The typical glass thermometer exploits the temperature dependency of a liquid's density to assess the environmental temperature based on the liquid's height in a glass capillary. Mercury-based glass thermometers are the most well-known representatives.
The working range is from −200 to 600 °C and the accuracy varies from 0.005 °C for laboratory thermometers to up to 4 °C in commercial applications.410 The method is cheap, easy to use, and has a basically noise-free signal; however, it delivers slow response times and is a purely analogue measurement. While it is highly suited for basic laboratory investigations of novel electrolytes – in particular due to the chemical resistance of glass – its analogue readout renders it unfeasible for computer-based in situ monitoring. Furthermore, such sensors exhibit a lower accuracy in cases where the glass capillary homogeneity is corrupted or the thermal expansion of the capillary is not properly considered.


3.7.2. Resistance based (thermoresistors and thermistors). 
Several materials exhibit a linear relationship of their electrical resistance with temperature in certain temperature ranges. Among metals, the most reliable material for this purpose is platinum. It is used as a standard for temperature measurement with a working range from −260 to 962 °C and an accuracy ranging from approximately 0.002 °C to 0.007 °C.410 In general the working range of such thermoresistors lies between −260 and 1064 °C. Semiconductors are used for temperature measurements as well. Even though the working principle of semiconductor-based devices is the same, they are specifically called thermistors. Thermistors are easy to fabricate and can be manufactured in bead, disk, rod, washer, and flake form, where forms of bead and disks can reach diameters between 70 and 10 μm.410 However, their accuracy is in the order of 1 °C for commercial applications and 0.01 to 0.05 °C in laboratory studies. These devices work in the range from −100 to 700 °C and their accuracy seems to be suitable for flow battery applications.410 However, in contrast to thermoresistors, thermistors are prone to signal drifts originating from temperature-induced changes in the semiconductor structure and, therefore, necessitate frequent recalibration. All electrical resistance-based sensors deliver a fast response time, digital readouts, and are found in the medium-to-low price segment.


3.7.3. Thermocouple. 
In a system with two contacting first order conductors, a thermal gradient causes an electromotive force (emf). This phenomenon is called Seebeck effect and it is used in thermocouples that are widespread in the field of thermometry. Thermocouples usually work in the range from −270 to 2300 °C with accuracies ranging from 0.5 to 2 °C.410 They are relatively cheap and demonstrate a fast digital response. If thermocouples are working at high temperatures, decalibration can occur because of annealing and subsequent changes in the structure of the sensor alloys. However, the temperature range in FB applications is not expected to cause such problems.


3.7.4. Radiation thermometers. 
In cases, where a measured sample can damage a sensor, non-invasive techniques can be used. Most of them utilize the detection and analysis of electromagnetic radiation emitted or influenced by a sample and are based on the sensing of infrared radiation, optical absorption or emission, scattering, and luminescence. The infrared sensors represent the most widespread variant. Modelling of radiation of a black body enables to assess the temperature in a range between −40 and 2000 °C. On the other hand, the signal is prone to distortion by thermal signals from the sample environment, the medium through which the radiation is transmitted, and the measurement device itself.410 Consequently, the accuracy is rather low: for an infrared sensor, a typical measurement uncertainty is 2 °C, while for other radiative thermometers error values as high as 15% are not uncommon. All radiation-based sensors demonstrate fast response times and are rather expensive in comparison to the aforementioned methods.


3.7.5. Applications in FB literature. 
In most of the temperature studies for flow batteries analyzed for this review, the kind of utilized sensor is not stated or it is unknown but included in the thermostat or utilized commercial heating bath setup.10,14,71,88,151,155,158,185,194,212,327,371,412–438 Literature resources in which the sensor is specified most often utilize thermocouples.64,230,328,439–442 However, a few examples for the application of platinum-based thermoresistors were also found.443,444 The temperature research in FBs is mostly focused on aqueous full-vanadium systems and aims at broadening the stability of the electrolyte under higher temperatures and finding the optimum working temperatures.14,71,88,135,151,155,185,194,230,327,328,416,417,423,425,428,431,434,436,437,440,444 Similar attempts can be found in particular for zinc-based flow batteries212,340,412,415,418–422,426,443,445 as well as other inorganic,427,430,432,433,442 organic aqueous-10,64,414,438 and ionic liquid-based446 electrolytes. Some reports investigate the stability and viable temperatures for Na–K eutectic-based FBs,439 deep eutectic systems,424 sodium–sulfur FBs,435 and FBs utilizing only the concentration gradient of sodium chloride in water.429 Research on organic solvent-based electrolytes is rather emphasizing on the temperature dependencies of kinetic characteristics and solubility investigations of the redox-active substances.371,372
In addition, it should be noted that the sensor position is highly important for the obtained results. In FB studies, sensors have been placed in the electrolyte tanks,230,328,440,442 along the tubing (T-fittings),64 outside the battery (thermostat, or sensor inside an oil or sand bath),9,156,194,212,327,371,412,413,417,418,420,421,423,425,426,428,429,431,432,434,436,437,441,446 and inside an electric heating plate (no flow battery operation included).340 A general judgement about the reliability of temperature measurements in all aforementioned cases is impossible; however, it can be stated that temperature reading is significantly dependent on sensor placement. The data is expected to be more reliable, when the sensor is placed in contact with an electrolyte, e.g., in T-fitting.64 Despite its importance, the topic is insufficiently investigated and, thus, requires further attention.
Based on the comprehensive review by Childs et al.410 and under consideration of the relative costs, the commercial availability, the external disturbance factors, the reproducibility, the accuracy, the response time, and the interesting working range (i.e., −35 to 80 °C), thermocouples, metal-based thermoresistors, and thermistors seem to be the best choice for FB electrolyte monitoring. However, thermistors demonstrate potential drift with time and, hence, are not suited for long-term measurements without frequent re-calibration.

4. Electrolyte state and stability assessment
4.1. State-of-charge measurement & monitoring
The state-of-charge of a flow battery electrolyte usually is defined as the ratio of charge stored in the corresponding electrolyte with respect to its total capacity. This translates to the concentration-based definition, eqn (2.22), given in Table 1. This definition represents the true SOC of a single flow battery electrolyte, SOCE. It should be noted that this definition is rigorously valid only for FB electrolytes where only two redox species are present. While this comprises the vast majority of the state-of-the-art FB electrolytes, more complex definitions need to be applied if reactants with several redox states are utilized. Since QB depends on several factors (e.g., temperature, applied current, individual electrolyte SOCs, etc.) the overall battery state-of-charge, SOCB, as defined in eqn (2.11) (Table 1) usually differs from the SOCE. In particular, SOCB depends on the individual SOCE as well as the capacity QE of each electrolyte and, thus, varies with any imbalance between the both half-cells. This needs to be considered, if individual half-cell SOCs are used for the battery management.
While early approaches for the SOC monitoring relied on the determination of SOCB, most of the stability and performance parameters of a FB depend on the SOC of the individual electrolytes in the half-cells.23,37,43,447,448 As a consequence, finding a reliable method for the measurement and monitoring of SOCE is regarded as one of the key issues for FB commercialization.447,449 In addition to the performance benefits that can be obtained from a proper SOC monitoring,148,175,194 fundamental problems can arise if electrolyte SOCs are not properly monitored as exemplified by the aforementioned report of Reynard and co-workers.25,450 Although the SOCB was monitored in this flow battery installation, the safety issue caused by electrolyte imbalances remained unrecognized since the SOCE values of the half-cells were unknown.
A remarkable number of methods for the SOC estimation of flow batteries and flow battery electrolytes have been reported in the scientific literature, mainly after 2008. The slowly increasing number of articles published per year underlines the increasingly recognized importance of this topic. A comprehensive review of the investigated techniques is provided in the following sections. Furthermore, Table 6 briefly summarizes the different techniques and their key properties.
Table 6 Summarizing table for SOC and SOH measurement and monitoring methods




Method
Applicability
Comments from the authors





State-of-charge (see Section 4.1)



Coulomb counting (4.1.1)

Ex situ and in situ
Its simplicity and accuracy for systems with high coulombic efficiency in short-term measurements makes it suitable for the online calibration of other SOC monitoring methods;14,198,451–455 prone to severe error accumulation over time and, thus, should not be used as a stand-alone monitoring method; requires exact knowledge of the starting SOC.


Titration (4.1.2)

Ex situ

Reliable and accurate but time-consuming method; is frequently utilized and widely accepted as a reliable validation method to evaluate the estimation errors of other SOC measurement techniques.6,160,169,170,230,456–458


Open circuit voltage (OCV) (4.1.3)

Ex situ and in situ
One of the standard methods; applicable in a wide range of electrolytes; moderate absolute errors of <5%, temperature- and concentration-dependencies as well as the requirement for initial calibration and frequent recalibrations limit its reliability in industrial applications.8,14,25,140,175,340,455,458–462


Spectroscopy and photometry (4.1.4)

Ex situ and in situ
Wide-spread applicability to different RFB chemistries; a thorough understanding of the underlying solution chemistry at different concentrations of the analyte is required in combination with accurate calibration under all possible combinations of electrolyte compositions, total concentrations, and temperatures;12,159–167,169–171,186,196–199,227,228,242,271,463–468 low absolute errors of ≤3% were reported.165,166


Conductivity (4.1.5)

Ex situ and in situ
Requires calibration under all possible combinations of concentrations and temperatures; applicable in systems with linear conductivity-SOC dependence;105,159,242,328,340,469,470 relative errors of ≤1% were reported.328


Viscosity and density (4.1.5)

Ex situ and in situ
Requires calibration under all possible combinations of concentrations and temperatures; is used in systems with linear viscosity/density-SOC dependence; viscosity-based measurements can be performed in situ based on pressure drop sensors which simplifies the sensor system but it decreases accuracy causing up to 10% absolute deviation from SOC values measured by OCV;9,14,242,340,471 RMSD of ≤4% were reported.14


Refractive index (4.1.5)

Ex situ and in situ
Requires calibration under all possible combinations of concentrations and temperatures; high refractive index sensitivity is needed for method application;340,457,472 measurement accuracy for SOC determination was not evaluated versus standard methods and, thus, remains unknown.


Acoustic properties (4.1.5)

Ex situ and in situ
Requires calibration under all possible combinations of concentrations and temperatures; measurement of acoustic wave velocity is strongly dependent on temperature, but measurement of acoustic attenuation was temperature-independent for VRFB electrolytes;230,456 moderate absolute errors of ≤5% were reported.230


Amperometry (4.1.6)

Ex situ and in situ
No calibration required; only temperature- and concentration-independent method reported so far; enables measurement of electrolyte state-of-charge; demonstrated so far only for VRFB, ferri-/ferrocyanide, and vanadium electrolytes but applicability to a wide range of RFB electrolytes is anticipated; requires that both redox states are in solution; absolute RMSDs ≤3% reported.455


Model-based methods (4.1.7)

In situ

Requires calibration under all possible combinations of concentrations and temperatures; usually do not demand additional sensor hardware; therefore, considered to be cost-efficient; requires accurate RFB models and comes at relatively high computational demand; possible to predict not only the battery SOC but also the concentration imbalances, crossover flux, and the attainable battery capacity; cannot deliver half-cell resolution when performed alone and, hence, electrolyte SOCs remain unknown; temperature influence is insufficiently investigated;476–495 low RMSDs of ≤1% were reported.481,485,487,491–493

Redox titration

Ex situ

Is used to measure substance concentrations and, hence, can be applied to measure SOH; reliable with moderate accuracy (5%159), but time-consuming method without online measurement capability.6,160,168,169,345,359


OCV

In situ

The method is focused on SOC monitoring but novel fitting procedures combined with coulomb counting enable in situ capacity and SOH determination on both battery and electrolyte level.14,455


Spectroscopy and photometry

Ex situ and in situ
Is used to measure substance concentrations; hence, can be applied to measure SOH. Requires dilution of electrolyte or very thin cuvettes; for in situ measurements a combination with independent methods for SOC measurement is required to estimate the SOH.12,162,166,180,271


Material properties based
Depends on the property
SOC can be measured by means of conductivity, viscosity, density, refractive index and acoustic properties, hence these techniques can be potentially applied for SOH readings.159,328,340,457

Ex situ and in situ
SOH measurement for the stability assessment utilizing steady-state amperometry at microelectrodes was reported for diluted electrolytes.501


Bulk cell cycling

In situ

Galvanostatic and potentiostatic cycling experiments in bulk cells enable battery capacity and SOH determination via coulomb counting; strongly related to stability and lifetime assessment.11,15,23,43,271

Require base of essential parameters like electrode surface area, diffusion coefficients, rate constants, reaction schemes; have the potential of application for ex situ SOH measurement.14,15,43,229,263,502,503

In situ

For some of the model-based state-of-charge monitoring methods a co-estimation of the battery SOH or the battery capacity was reported;484,485,491 usually cannot predict individual electrolyte capacities; the only exception to this introduced two OCV cells, which mitigates one of the main advantages of this method (i.e., no additional hardware requirement).478







Stability and lifetime assessment (see Section 4.3)
